URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS52351849	2019-03-18 22:17:58	31269	species	Red Crossbill	Loxia curvirostra			10				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003	US-NV_943	33			Buck Spring Canyon, Spring Mtns.	L514246	P	36.3399842	-115.7789040	2008-04-06	21:00:00	obsr89980	S3773211	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	360	7.242			1		0	1	0		ZTHA: --[Describe in as much detail as possible the bird which you saw without referring to field guides.  Emphasize particularly the marks used to identify the bird, but include a complete description of the bird.  Include size, overall shape, plumage and color pattern; details on shape or size of bill, wings, and tail.  Describe what you are actually observing in the field.]--The bird was a fairly large raptor with fairly rectangular, parallel edged wings and a long tail.  Overall the plumage was black on the body, underwing coverts, and tail.  The flight feathers were silvery gray, underneath.  At first glance the bird was quickly dimissed as a Turkey Vulture to which it closely resembled in size, shape, and plumage.  When I put my binoculars on it a single bold solid white subterminal band was clearly visible on the underside of the otherwise black tail.  ------[Describe the bird's overall behavior:]--The bird came gliding across the sky from the direction of the crest of the Spring Mts./Bonanza Peak area.  If glided quickly and directly on a very slight dihedral.  Within 15 seconds or so it disappeared behind a ridge to the northwest.----[Describe the habitat in which you observed the bird:]--Area is dominated by farily dry Pinon-Juniper woodland with contiguous stands of mature Ponderosa and a few White Firs following the more mesic drainage bottoms.  Because the bird was gliding high over a broad area I can comment of specific micro-habitat.  The sighting occurred very near a fairly wet spring with a particularly mesic ponderosa canyon directly above.  The area below the crest, from which the bird may have came, is dominated by Ponderosa, White Fir, and possibly a few Bristlecones (as IDed at great length through binoculars).--------[Describe the bird's calls or sounds, if heard, including method of delivery, i.e., from perch, in flight, etc.:]  None given--------[List similar species and how you eliminated them:]--As stated, my initial impression was of a Turkey Vulture.  Upon closer inspection this was easily eliminated based on the solid white subterminal band on the underside of the tail.  The fast direct flight and shallow dihedral were also somewhat characteristic of a TV.  Common Black-hawk was easily eliminated based on the long tail, relatively narrow and straight wings, black tail tip, and distinctly two-toned underwings.  The flight feathers were too dark and the tail pattern all wrong for a dark rough-legged.  The bold single white tail band was inconsistent with any type of Red-tailed hawk including Harlan's.  The flight feathers were also too dark gray/silver and the wing shape wrong (too straight)------[Prior experience with this and similar species:]--I have extensive experience with Zone-tailed Hawk from New Mexico, Arizona, and once Texas.  I've lived for four years in southern NM where I focused much birding effort on the southwestern mountains where the species borders on common.  I have seen it many times in the Chiricahuas and other ranges of SE Arizona as well.  I am also very experienced with Turkey Vulture.----[Optical equipment:]--Swift Eaglet 10X42 roof prism binoculars----
